Therefore, when your treatment progresses in this manner, and you see material emerging from the sinus opening, then know that the sinus is already adhering or is close to adherence; then, release it in the morning or on the third day, and when you uncover the wound, replace the cloth and renew the ointment.
Si autem egreditur ex Sinu post spacium longum uirus subtile, tunc non desperes de adhaerentia eius, nam sanatio eius appropinquat, quoniam ex hominibus sunt qui tardant generationem carnis in uulneribus suis, et ex eis sunt qui agunt econtrario illius.	However, if thin discharge comes out from the sinus after a long interval, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, since among people, there are those who are slow in generating flesh in their wounds, and others who act contrary to this.
Si autem egreditur ex Sinu post dies multos pus indigestum, tunc scias quod sinus non adhaeret.	But if undigested pus emerges from the sinus after many days, then know that the sinus is not adhering.
Si ergo tardatur adhaerentia Sinus, et prolongatur res eius: tunc adde in exiccatione medicinarum, uerum faciant medici, ut sint medicinae in substantia sua humidae, et in uirtute sua siccae: sicut unguentum dyafinicon, quando additur in eo superfluitas additionis ex zegi.	Therefore, if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its matter is prolonged, then increase the drying quality of the medicines; let physicians make the medicines moist in substance but dry in their effect, like dyafinicon ointment, when an excess of zegi is added to it.
Et ex magis ultimo quo curatur, est, ut accipias unguentum dyafinicon, et soluatur cum oleo rosato, et roretur super ipsum uinum antiquum aequale in substantia sua.	And among the more final treatments, take dyafinicon ointment, dissolve it with rose oil, and sprinkle it with old wine in equal measure.
Deinde confice ipsum cum eo, et utere ipso.	Then prepare it with this and use it.
Et iam facit simile huic actioni, quando non est tibi praesens unguentum dyafinicon, mel, quando decoquis ipsum, donec ingrossetur, et uteris eo.	And similar to this procedure, when dyafinicon ointment is not available, is honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it.
Aut accipe myrrham, et aloen, et olibanum: tere totum, et pulueriza illud super mel dum est super ignem.	Or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense; grind it all, and sprinkle it over honey while it is over the fire.
Deinde line super ipsum pannum, et stringe ipsum super Sinum: et line cum melle quod decoquisti, donec ingrossatum est: et pulueriza super ipsum species, et pone super ipsum pannum, et stringe eum.	Then spread it on a cloth and bind it over the sinus; also spread it with the honey you boiled until thickened, sprinkle spices over it, place it on a cloth, and bind it.
Et quandoque administratur in illo aristolochia longa, et radices lilii coelestis, et est lilium: et farina herbi, et centaurea.	And sometimes long aristolochia, and the roots of heavenly lily, which is a lily, and herb flour, and centaury are applied to it.
Utere eis simplicibus, aut aggregatis, tere ea, et cribella ipsa: et pulueriza super mel quod narraui, et utere eo.	Use them simply or combined, grind them, sift them, and sprinkle them over the honey as I mentioned, and use it.
Quod si prolongatur res Sinus, et non sanatur hac curatione: tunc scias, quod in profundo eius est corruptio: aut iam facit impressionem in ossibus, aut in reliquis membris duris, aut ligamentis: tunc cura ipsum curatione Fistularum, secundum quod praemissum est.	But if the matter of the sinus is prolonged and it is not healed by this treatment, then know that there is corruption in its depth, or it is already affecting the bones, or the other hard parts, or ligaments; then treat it with the cure for fistulas, as previously mentioned.
¶ De cura Panaritii et unguis attritae, et incisione digiti additi, et sectione.	On the care of panaritium, injured nail, and incision of an extra finger, and section.
Caput XCI.	Chapter 91.
Panaritium est apostema quod nascitur sub ungue pollicis manus, aut pedis: et fortasse nascitur in reliquis digitis.	Panaritium is an abscess that forms under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and perhaps it occurs on other fingers.
Cum ergo prolongatur res eius, et negligitur curato eius, et apostematur apostemate calido: aut corrumpitur, et emittit pus, donec corrodit radicem unguis: et fortasse corrumpit eam totam, et fortasse peruenit corruptio ad os, donec est ei odor foetidus: et fit extremitas digiti lata, et fit color eius fuscus.	When its condition is prolonged, and treatment is neglected, it becomes a warm abscess or decays and releases pus, until it corrodes the nail root; it may destroy it entirely, and sometimes the decay reaches the bone, producing a foul odor, and the tip of the finger becomes swollen and dark-colored.
Quando ergo tu curas ipsum, cum eo quod diximus in diuisione, et non confert curatio tua: tunc oportet ut incidas cum ferro totam superfluitatem quae remansit ex ungue.	When you are treating it as we described in the previous section, and your treatment is not effective, then you must cut away with an iron all the excess that remains of the nail.
Deinde cauteriza uulnus post illud.	Then cauterize the wound afterward.
Cauterium enim iuuatiuum est ualde.	For cauterization is very helpful.
Si uero os est sanum, et est unguis etiam sana, et additiones uacuae unguis iam comprimunt carnem ad interiora, et incipiunt pungere ipsam, et laedunt eam: tunc oportet ut ponas Radium subtilem sub angulo unguis quae pungit carnem, et impelle eam sursum: et incide eam carnem cum facilitate, et pone super illud quod remanet de carne ex medicinis comburentibus, corrosiuis, donec remoueatur totum eius.	If, however, the bone is sound and the nail is also healthy, and the empty extensions of the nail press the flesh inward, begin to prick it, and injure it, then you should place a thin stick under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh and push it upward; cut away the flesh with ease, and apply burning or corrosive medicines to what remains of the flesh until all of it is removed.
Deinde cura illud cum unguentis, donec sanetur.	Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Si autem corruptio iam imprimit in os, tunc oportet ut incidas illud os, et extrahas ipsum: non enim sanatur unquam dum permanet in eo os corruptum.	But if the decay has already affected the bone, then you must cut away that bone and remove it, for it will never heal as long as the corrupted bone remains.
Quod si uides corrosionem et corruptionem incedere in digitum, tunc incide eum in uno nodorum, secundum quod praemissa est eius rememoratio.	If you see the corrosion and decay spreading into the finger, then cut it at one of the joints, as previously described.
Deinde cura ipsum, donec sanetur.	Then treat it until it heals.
Si autem accidat ungui attritio, aut percussio: et contingit in ea dolor uehemens, tunc oportet ut phlebotomes infirmum in primis.	If the nail is damaged or struck, causing intense pain, then first it is necessary to perform phlebotomy on the patient.
Deinde seca unguem cum Spatumili acuto sectione transuersa de sursum ad uisum.	Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula in a transverse incision from above toward the visible part.
Et custodi, ne sectio perueniat ad carnem quae est sub ungue, nam tu facies accidere per illud super infirmum dolorem uehementem, et erit caussa ad natiuitatem carnis additae in loco.	Be careful not to let the incision reach the flesh under the nail, for you would cause intense pain for the patient and lead to the growth of extra flesh in that spot.
Deinde cura locum cum eo quod sedat dolores.	Then treat the area with something that soothes pain.
Digitus uero additus qui nascitur in quibusdam manibus hominum, fortasse est carnosus totus: et fortasse sunt in quibusdam eorum ossa, et fortasse est in eo unguis: et quidam eorum nascitur in radice iuncturae, aut est natiuitas eius in quibusdam nodis digiti.	An extra finger, which appears in some people's hands, may be entirely fleshy, or in some cases contain bones, or may even have a nail; and some grow from the root of a joint or originate at certain joints of the finger.
Ille ergo qui nascitur in quibusdam nodis digiti, non mouetur: et ille qui nascitur apud iuncturam digiti, fortasse mouetur.	One that grows at certain finger joints is immobile, while one that grows near a finger joint may be mobile.
Eius ergo qui ex eis est carnosus, sectio eius facilis est.	Thus, the section of those that are fleshy is easy.
Quod est, ut incidas ipsum apud radicem suam cum Spatumili lato.	That is, to cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
Eius autem cuius natiuitas est in radice iuncturae curatio, est difficilis.	However, the treatment of one that originates at the root of a joint is difficult.
Abstine ergo ab incisione eius.	Therefore, refrain from incising it.
In illo uero qui nascitur in digito apud ultimum nodorum, tunc oportet ut incidas in primis carnem eius incisione rotunda usque ad os.	But for one that grows on the finger near the last joint, you should first cut its flesh in a circular incision down to the bone.
Deinde serra os cum una illarum Serrarum conuenientium ad illud.	Then saw the bone with one of those saws suited for it.
Deinde cura ipsum, donec sanetur.	Then treat it until it heals.
Consolidatio autem quae accidit digitis adinuicem, multotiens accidit aut ex natiuitate hominis, id est, nascitur cum eo, aut a consolidatione uulneris, aut combustione ignis, et similibus.	Fusion of the fingers, which often occurs either by birth — that is, they are born with it — or by the healing of a wound, or by burning with fire, and similar causes.
Oportet ergo ut seces illam consolidationem, donec redeant digiti secundum formam suam naturalem.	It is necessary, therefore, to cut that fusion until the fingers return to their natural shape.
Deinde pone inter eos licinia, aut pannos infusos in oleo rosato: ut non consolidentur uelociter, et separa inter eos: aut pone inter eos laminam plumbi subtilem, donec consolidetur locus secundum quod oportet.	Then place linen strips or cloths soaked in rose oil between them, so they do not fuse again quickly, and keep them separated; or place a thin lead sheet between them until the area consolidates as needed.
Et similiter si accidat consolidatio quibusdam digitis cum palma, tunc seca illam consolidationem, secundum quod praeparatur et conuenit figurae membri.	Similarly, if some fingers are fused with the palm, then cut that fusion according to what is prepared and suited to the shape of the limb.
¶ De incisione Vitium.	On the incision of the disease.
Caput XCII.	Chapter 92.
Uites sunt Venae inuolutae, grossae, plenae superfluitatibus melancholicis quae accidunt in pluribus membris corporis: et plurimum euentus earum est in cruribus, praecipue in cruribus iter agentium et laborantium, et portantium.	Veins are twisted, thick, and full of melancholic superfluities that appear in various parts of the body: and mostly, they occur in the legs, especially in those who travel, labor, and carry loads.
Oportet ergo ut administres excussionem corporis ex cholera nigra aliquotiens excussione forti.	Therefore, it is necessary to administer an expulsion of black bile from the body with a strong expulsion at times.
Deinde phlebotoma patientis eas basilicam.	Then perform a phlebotomy on the basilic vein of the patient.
Curatio uero earum cum ferro, est secundum duos modos.	Their treatment with iron is in two ways.
Vnus eorum est, ut secentur, et extrahatur sanguis niger.	One of these is that they should be cut, and black blood should be extracted.
Et modus alter est, ut eleuetur Vena, et extrahatur tota.	The other way is to elevate the vein and remove it entirely.
Sectio uero eius est secundum hunc modum.	The cutting of it is in this manner.
Embroca crus in primis cum aqua calida multum, donec resoluatur sanguis grossus faeculentus.	First, soak the leg with a lot of hot water until the thick, foul blood is dissolved.
Deinde stringe crus infirmi a superiori parte coxae eius, usque ad inferiorem partem genu cum uitta.	Then bind the patient's leg from the upper part of the thigh down to the lower part of the knee with a band.
Deinde seca uenam in loco uno, aut duobus, aut tribus sectione ampla.	Then cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.
Deinde eleua, id est, extrahe sanguinem in manu tua ab inferiori parte cruris, usque ad sursum, et a superiori oportet ut phlebotomes infirmum in primis.	Then lift, that is, extract the blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upward, and from the upper part perform a phlebotomy on the patient initially.
Deinde seca unguem cum usque inferius: donec egrediatur ex sanguine quantitas quam uides sufficere, et quam tolerat uirtus infirmi.	Then cut down to the nail until an amount of blood comes out that you see as sufficient and within the tolerance of the patient's strength.
Postea liga eam: et praecipe ei, ut abstineat a cibis generantibus choleram nigram: et itera euacuationem et phlebotomiam, quando implentur Venae, et nocet illud infirmo.	Afterward, bind it, and instruct the patient to abstain from foods that generate black bile: and repeat the evacuation and phlebotomy when the veins are filled, and this harms the patient.
Eleuatio autem eius est secundum hunc modum.	Its elevation is in this way.
Rade crus infirmi, si in ea sunt pili multi.	Shave the patient’s leg if there are many hairs on it.
Deinde intromitte ipsum in balneum: aut embroca ipsum in crure eius cum aqua calida, donec rubeat, et exuberentur Venae: aut exercitetur exercitio forti, si non est ei praesens balneum, donec calefiat membrum.	Then immerse it in a bath, or bathe it with hot water on the leg until it reddens and the veins swell, or have the patient engage in vigorous exercise if a bath is not available until the limb is heated.
Deinde seca cutem in directo uenae sectione per longitudinem: aut in ultimo eius apud genu, aut in inferiori parte eius apud calcaneum.	Then cut the skin along the vein in a longitudinal section, either at its end near the knee or in its lower part near the heel.
Deinde aperi cutem cum uncinis, et excoria uenam ab omni parte, donec appareat sensui: et est apud apparitionem eius, quod uides eam rubeam ad nigredinem decliuem.	Then open the skin with hooks and strip the vein on all sides until it becomes visible to touch: and upon its appearance, you see it red with a blackish tint.
Cum ergo uadit ex cute, uides eam albam quasi sit chorda.	When it thus goes out from the skin, you see it white as if it were a cord.
Deinde intromitte sub ea Radium, donec quando eleuatur et egreditur de cute: suspende eam cum uncino caeco, leni.	Then insert a probe beneath it, so that when it is elevated and exits the skin, suspend it with a gentle, blunt hook.
Deinde seca sectionem aliam prope illam sectionem tribus digitis.	Then make another incision near that incision, three fingers apart.
Deinde excoria cutem a superiori parte uenae, donec appareat.	Then strip the skin from the upper part of the vein until it becomes visible.
Deinde eleua eam cum Radiis sicut fecisti, et suspende eam cum uncino alio, sicut fecisti in primis.	Then elevate it with probes as you did before and suspend it with another hook as you did initially.
Deinde seca sectionem aliam, aut sectiones plures, si indiges illo.	Then cut another incision, or several incisions, if needed.
Deinde eleua, et incide ipsam ultima sectione apud calcaneum.	Then elevate and cut it with a final incision near the heel.
Deinde extrahe eam ad sectionem quae est supra eam.	Then draw it out to the section that is above it.
Et fac illud, donec extrahas eam ex sectione quae est superior, uel altior sectionibus omnibus, donec quando egreditur tota: tunc incide eam.	And do this until you extract it from the section that is higher, or from all the incisions, until it is fully drawn out: then cut it.
Si autem non obedit tibi ad extrahendum, et eleuandum: tunc intromitte acum cum filo forti duplo, et liga eam, et extrahe ipsam: aut intromitte sub ea Radium: et reuolue manum tuam ad omnem partem, donec egrediatur.	But if it does not respond to extraction and elevation, then insert a needle with a strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull it out, or insert a probe beneath it, and turn your hand to every side until it comes out.
Et caue, ne incidatur.	And be careful not to cut it.
Nam si incidatur, fit difficile tibi eleuare eam ualde, et ingreditur super infirmum ex ea nocumentum.	For if it is cut, it becomes very difficult to elevate, and it causes harm to the patient.
Cum ergo extraxeris eam totam: tunc pone super locum uulnerum lanam submersam in uino et oleo rosato, aut oleo: et cura ipsum, donec sanetur.	When you have extracted it entirely, place wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, over the wounds, and treat the patient until healed.
Quod si Vitis est contracta, habens tortuositatem cui sit inuolutio ad partes, et non est sicut diximus secundum rectitudinem: tunc oportet ut findas super eam ad omnem partem tortuositatis eius, et loca inuolutionis ipsius.	But if the vein is contracted, having twists and bends, and is not as we have said in a straight line, then it is necessary to cut it over each twist and bend.
Deinde suspende eam cum uncinis, donec eleues eam totam: et caue apud sectionem tuam super eam ne incidas uenam, aut uulneres: nam difficile fiet super te eleuare, uel extrahere eam.	Then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it entirely, and be careful near your incision on it not to cut or injure the vein, for it will become difficult for you to elevate or extract it.
Conserua ergo prudentiam tuam.	Therefore, maintain your caution.
Forma Incidentis quo sanatur Vitis.	The form of the incision by which the vein is healed.
Forma Vncini caeci.	The form of the blunt hook.
Non est ei curuitas sicut reliquis Vncinis, neque est acutae extremitatis, ut non uulneret uenam: et est grossae flectuositatis, lenis: quoniam si esset subtilis, incideret uenam subtilitate sua: imo sit ei superflua grossitudo, sicut diximus.	It does not have the curve like other hooks, nor a sharp end, so as not to wound the vein; it is thick and smooth, for if it were slender, it would cut the vein by its thinness; indeed, let it have extra thickness, as we have said.
De extractione Venae cruris.	On the extraction of the vein of the leg.
Caput XCIII.	Chapter 93.
Haec Vena generatur in cruribus in terris calidis, sicut Alizegi, et terra Arabum, et in terris aridis, calidis, paucae teneritatis, id est, in quibus non nascitur herba, neque arbor.	This vein forms in the legs in hot lands, like Alizegi, the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands with little tenderness, that is, where no grass or trees grow.
Et fortasse generatur in locis aliis corporis praeter crura.	And it may perhaps form in other places of the body besides the legs.
Et generatio quidem eius est a putrefactione quae accidit sub cute, sicut accidunt intra corpora serpentes et uermes ascarides, et uermes generati inter cutem et carnem.	Its formation indeed is due to decay that occurs beneath the skin, similar to how serpents and ascarid worms occur within bodies and worms form between the skin and flesh.
Et signum incaeptionis euentus huius Venae est, quod accidit in crure inflammatio uehemens.	The sign of the beginning of this vein’s formation is that a violent inflammation occurs in the leg.
Deinde uesicatur locus.	Then the area becomes blistered.
Deinde incipit Vena exire ex loco illius uesicationis, quasi sit radix plantae, aut animal.	Then the vein begins to emerge from the site of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant or an animal.
Cum ergo apparet ex ea extremitas eius, tunc oportet ut inuoluas super ipsam frustum plumbi, cuius pondus sit ex drachma keil, usque ad drachmas II et noda ipsam: et dimitte plumbum suspensum ex crure.	When its end appears, you must wrap around it a piece of lead weighing from one to two drachmas, and knot it, letting the lead hang from the leg.
Quotiens ergo egreditur ex ea aliquid ad exteriora, inuolue illud in plumbo, et noda illud.	Thus, whenever something from it comes outward, wrap it in lead and knot it.
Quod si prolongatur multum, tunc incide partem eius, et inuolue residuum.	If it becomes too long, then cut part of it and wrap the remainder.
Et non incidas eam radicitus antequam egrediatur tota.	And do not cut it at the root before it is entirely extracted.
Quoniam si incidis, contrahitur, et ingreditur in carnem: et facit accidere apostema, et putredinem in loco, et ulcus malum.	For if you cut it, it contracts and goes into the flesh, causing an abscess, decay in the area, and a bad ulcer.
Quapropter oportet, ut suauiter tractetur, et extrahatur paulatim, donec egrediatur ex ultimo sui, et non remaneat ex ea aliquid in corpore: et ex hac quidem Vena egreditur in quibusdam hominibus quod est in longitudine sua quinque palmorum et decem.	Therefore, it must be handled gently and extracted gradually until its very end is reached, leaving nothing of it in the body; in some people, this vein emerges with a length of five and ten palms.
Et iam peruenit ad me, quia exiuit uiro ex XX palmis.	It has even been reported to me that it came out twenty palms long in one man.
Si autem inciditur tibi in hora medicationis tuae: tunc intromitte radium in foramine eius, et perfora eam foramine longo, cum corpore, donec euacuetur totum quod est in ea ex materia: et administra putrefactionem loci cum medicinis diebus aliquot.	If it is cut during your treatment, then insert a probe into its hole, pierce it with a long opening, along with the body, until all the matter within is evacuated, and treat the decay in the area with medicines for a few days.
Deinde cura ipsum curatione apostematum.	Then treat it as you would for abscesses.
Et haec quidem Vena quandoque habet ramos multos, praecipue quando apparet in iunctura pedis, aut in pede ipso: et accidunt ei orificia multa, et egreditur ex omni orificio ramus.	This vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot itself, and it has many openings, with a branch emerging from each opening.
Cura ergo ipsam, sicut diximus in diuisione, et cum eo quod praemissum est.	Therefore, treat it as we have described in the section, and with what has been previously stated.
¶ De sectione super Vermes generatos sub cute, et dicitur Aegritudo bouina.	On the incision for worms generated under the skin, called the "Bovine Disease."
Caput XCIIII.	Chapter 94.
Haec Aegritudo nominatur in quadam terra apud nos Aegritudo bouina: propterea, quia multotiens accidit bouibus, et est uermis paruus unus, qui nascitur inter cutem et carnem.	This disease is called the "Bovine Disease" in a certain land among us, because it often occurs in cattle, and it is a small worm that forms between the skin and flesh.
Et uadit in corpore toto, ascendendo et descendendo: et ostenditur sensui apud ambulationem eius de membro ad membrum, donec disrumpit ubicumque disrumpit in cute locum, et egreditur.	And it moves throughout the whole body, ascending and descending, and becomes perceptible as it moves from one limb to another, until it eventually breaks through the skin and emerges wherever it ruptures.
Et generatio eius est ex putredine quorundam humorum.	Its formation is due to the decay of certain humors.
Sicut accidunt uermes et serpentes, id est, lumbrici, et ascarides in uentre.	Just as worms and serpents, that is, roundworms and ascarids, occur in the belly.
Et timetur ex malicia eius.	It is feared for its malignancy.
Quia quando ipse uadit in corpore, et eleuatur ad caput, et peruenit ad locum: tunc fortasse aperit in eo, et egreditur, et destruit oculum: et accidit illud multotiens.	For when it moves through the body and rises to the head, and reaches a place, it may rupture there, emerge, and destroy the eye; this happens frequently.
Cum ergo uis curare eum, et extrahere ipsum, tunc non fit illud nisi apud ambulationem eius, et apparitionem ipsius sensui.	Therefore, when you wish to treat and remove it, this can only be done when it is moving and visible to the senses.
Oportet ergo, ut stringas quod est supra eum, et quod est sub eo cum ligamento constrictione forti.	You must therefore bind tightly above and below it with a constricting band.
Deinde seca super ipsum, et extrahe eum.	Then make an incision over it and extract it.
Quod si profundatur in carne, et non inuenis ipsum: tunc pone super locum cauterium cum igne, donec moriatur.	But if it is deep in the flesh and cannot be found, then apply a cautery with fire over the area until it dies.
Et maius quod timetur, est corruptio eius, qua corrumpit oculum, sicut diximus.	The greatest fear is its corruption, by which it destroys the eye, as we have mentioned.
Si autem uides eum iam peruenisse in capite prope oculum, tunc stringe sub eo supercilium strictura bona.	If you see it has already reached the head near the eye, then bind tightly beneath it with a strong constriction on the eyebrow.
Deinde seca super ipsum, et extrahe eum.	Then make an incision over it and extract it.
Et oportet ut utatur infirmus mundificatione sui corporis, cum medicinis soluentibus humores putridos malos.	The patient must cleanse their body with medicines that dissolve foul, putrid humors.
Et caueat sibi a cibis generantibus putrefactionem.	And they should avoid foods that cause decay.
¶ De sectione super aegritudinem Nakir.	On the incision for the disease Nakir.
Caput XCV.	Chapter 95.
Haec aegritudo nominatur in terra nostra Nakir: et est dolor qui accidit in quibusdam membris: deinde permutatur de membro ad membrum.	This disease is called Nakir in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, then shifts from one limb to another.
Et ego quidem iam uidi ipsum, secundum quod narro tibi.	And I have indeed seen it, as I will tell you.
Vocatus sum ad mulierem infirmam in quibusdam uillis.	I was called to an ailing woman in a certain village.
Et detexit brachium suum, et uidi inflationem paruam in uena funis brachii.	She uncovered her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm cord.
Et postquam remansi hora una, uidi unam inflationem incedere cum focili, sicut incedit uermis, ascendendo ad humerum eius uelocius quam esse potest: ut sit sicut argentum uiuum, quando currit de loco ad locum.	After remaining there for an hour, I saw a swelling moving along like a worm, ascending to her shoulder faster than can be believed, like quicksilver running from one place to another.
Recessit ergo dolor ex illo loco, et fixus est in humero.	The pain then left that area and became fixed in the shoulder.
Deinde sedi hora una, et cucurrit in reliquum corpus, donec perueniret in brachium aliud.	Then, after sitting for another hour, it ran into the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Deinde narrauit mihi, quia reuoluebatur in toto corpore suo, secundum quod uidi.	Then she told me that it moved throughout her whole body, as I had seen.
Miratus sum ergo de uelocitate permutationis eius de membro ad membrum.	I was amazed at the speed of its movement from one limb to another.
Et ego quidem ante illud non uideram hanc aegritudinem oculis meis taliter secundum hunc modum.	I had not seen this illness with my own eyes in such a manner before.
Verumtamen uidi multos, qui inueniunt dolorem permutari de membro ad membrum: et non uidi ipsum oculis, sicut uidi eum in muliere.	However, I have seen many who experience pain moving from limb to limb, though I had not seen it with my eyes as I did in this woman.
Et ego non cogito illud esse, nisi quod est propterea, quod mulier fuit ex illis qui sunt ex uillis sicci corporis detectarum uenarum: hinc ergo apparet sensui illa uentositas permutata.	And I do not think this is the case unless it is because the woman was from those villages of people with dry bodies and exposed veins; thus, this shifting windiness appears to the senses.
Et est necesse, ut non appareant secundum hanc rationem in illis qui sunt deliciosi, et corporibus humidis, occultarum uenarum.	And it is necessary that this does not appear in those who are indulgent, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
Cum ergo uis curationem eius, et sentit patiens illud dolorem: tunc si est apparens tibi oculo, sicut diximus, tunc stringe supra ipsum, et sub ipso uelociter: et seca supra ipsum, donec egrediatur illa uentositas coartata: et cauteriza locum.	Therefore, when you wish to treat it and the patient feels the pain, if it is visible to you, as we have said, then bind tightly above and below it quickly; make an incision over it until the confined windiness exits, and cauterize the area.
Si autem non uides ipsum oculis tuis: tunc cura ipsum cum excussione corporis, et cum eo quod expellit uentositates, et carminat eas cum pillulis foetidis, et pillulis de serapino, et similibus.	But if it is not visible to your eyes, then treat it with body evacuation, and with what expels windiness, and soothes it with foul-smelling pills and pills of serapinum, and the like.
¶ De extractione Sagittarum.	On the extraction of arrows.
Caput XCVI.	Chapter 96.
Sagittae diuersificantur secundum species suas, et secundum loca in quibus cadunt ex corpore.	Arrows vary according to their types and according to the places in the body where they lodge.
Earum uero diuersitas est: quoniam ex eis sunt quae habent cuspides magnos, et paruos: et concauae, et surdae.	Their diversity is as follows: for among them are those that have large and small points, and those that are concave and deaf.
Et ex eis sunt quae habent tres angulos.	And among them are those that have three edges.
Et ex eis quibus sunt linguae.	And among them are those with tongues.
Et ex eis quibus sunt astellae.	And among them are those with stars.
Illae uero quae sunt secundum membra in quibus cadunt, est secundum duos modos.	Those that lodge in certain parts of the body fall into two categories.
Aut enim membra sunt ex membris principalibus concauis, sicut cerebrum, et cor, et epar, et pulmo, et renes, et intestina, et uesica, et eis similia.	Either they are in the principal hollow organs, such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and similar parts.
Quando ergo cadit in aliquo horum membrorum, et apparent tibi signa mortis, quae ego narro tibi post, tunc oportet ut deuites extractionem illius sagittae ex eis, nam mors consequitur illum in quo est, in pluribus dispositionibus.	Therefore, when it lodges in any of these parts, and signs of death appear to you, which I will tell you later, you must avoid extracting that arrow from them, for death follows in most cases.
Et quando non apparent tibi ista signa mala, et sagitta non est absconsa in profunditate membri, tunc extrahe ipsam, et cura uulnus.	And when these evil signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the part, then extract it and treat the wound.
Ex signis enim cerebri quando cadit in eo sagitta, et penetrat os, et egreditur siphac quod est super cerebrum, est: quoniam accidunt ex illo soda uehemens, et scotomia, et uertigo, et rubedo in oculis, et inflatio, et rubedo linguae, et spasmus, et permixtio intellectus, et uomitus cholerae.	Among the signs of the brain when an arrow lodges there, and it penetrates the bone, and the membrane over the brain emerges, are these: sudden seizures, dizziness, vertigo, redness in the eyes, swelling, redness of the tongue, spasms, mental confusion, and vomiting of bile.
Et fortasse egreditur sanguis ex naribus, aut auribus.	And sometimes blood comes out from the nose or ears.
Et fortasse abscinditur sermo, et destruitur uox.	And sometimes speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed.
Et egreditur ex loco uulneris humiditas alba similis pulti.	And a white moisture similar to porridge comes out of the wound site.
Et exit ex eo simile aquositati carnis.	And something similar to the watery discharge of flesh exits from it.
Si ergo apparent tibi ista signa, tunc abstine a curatione, et extrahe sagittam si non egreditur.	If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not come out.
Signa autem sagittae quando cadit in corde, sunt: quod ipsa est prope mammillam sinistram: et sentit cum ea quasi ipsa sit fixa in re dura, non in re uacua.	The signs of an arrow when it lodges in the heart are that it is near the left nipple, and it feels as if it is fixed in a hard object, not in an empty one.
Et fortasse sagittae est motus similis motui pulsus: et currit ex uulnere sanguis niger.	And sometimes the movement of the arrow resembles the beat of a pulse, and black blood flows from the wound.
Et sequitur illud frigus extremitatum, et sudor frigidus, et syncopis.	And coldness of the extremities follows, along with cold sweat and fainting.
Scias ergo, quod mors descendit procul dubio.	Know, therefore, that death approaches without a doubt.
Et signum sagittae quando uulnerat pulmonem, est exitus sanguinis spumosi ex uulnere: et uasa quae sequuntur collum, apostematur, et mutatur color infirmi.	The sign of an arrow wounding the lung is foamy blood coming from the wound, and the vessels following the neck become inflamed, and the patient's color changes.
Et expirat spiritum magnum, altum: et quaerit attractionem aeris frigidi.	And he breathes a deep, high breath, and seeks the attraction of cold air.
Quod si cadat sagitta in uelamen, quod est in pectore: tunc est prope costas paruas: et fit anhelitus magnus cum dolore uehementi, cum suspirio: et mouentur omnia membra humerorum.	But if the arrow lodges in the membrane in the chest, then it is near the small ribs, and there is great shortness of breath with intense pain, with sighing, and all the muscles of the shoulders move.
Et si cadat sagitta in stomachum, tunc fortasse egreditur ex uulnere ex cibo aliquid indigestum.	And if the arrow lodges in the stomach, then perhaps something undigested exits from the wound.
Et si cadit sagitta in Ventrem, et figitur in eo: egreditur aliquid stercoris ex uulnere, aut zirbum, aut intestinum quod iam disruptum est.	And if the arrow lodges in the abdomen and sticks there, something of feces exits from the wound, or bile, or intestine that is already ruptured.
Non est ergo spes in curatione eius, neque in extractione sagittae.	Therefore, there is no hope in curing it, nor in extracting the arrow.
Quod si sagitta cadat in Vesicam, et egrediatur urina: et procedit ex ea aliquid ad exteriora, et fortior sit dolor super infirmum: tunc scias, quia est mortale.	But if the arrow lodges in the bladder and urine comes out, and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger over the afflicted, then know that it is fatal.
Reliqua autem membra, sicut facies, et collum, et guttur, et spatula, et adiutorium, et spondiles dorsi, et furcula, et coxa, et crus, et eis similia ex membris saluantur secundum plures dispositiones: quando non inuenit sagitta arteriam, aut neruum: et non est sagitta uenenosa.	However, the remaining parts, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulder, auxiliary part, spinal vertebrae, clavicle, hip, leg, and similar parts of the body are preserved in most cases, as long as the arrow does not hit an artery or nerve and is not poisonous.
Et ego narro tibi quod uidi de esse harum sagittarum, ut significes per illud super curationem tuam.	And I tell you what I have observed concerning these arrows, so that you may apply it in your practice.
Quod est, quia cuidam uiro cecidit sagitta in lachrymali oculi eius, in radice nasi.	For instance, an arrow fell into a man’s tear duct at the root of his nose.
Extraxi ergo eam ipsi ex parte altera, sub adipe auris eius: et sanatus est, et non accidit ei in oculo eius nocumentum.	I therefore extracted it for him from the other side, under the fat of his ear, and he was healed, and no harm occurred to his eye.
Et extraxi sagittam aliam Iudaeo, quae cecidit ei in adipe oculi sui sub palpebra inferiori: et sagitta iam occultata erat, et non consecutus sum ex ea nisi extremitatem paruam eius, cum qua adhaeret in ligno suo: et erat sagitta magna ex sagittis arcuum Turcorum, cum ferro leni, in qua non sunt aures.	And I extracted another arrow for a Jew, which had lodged in the fat of his eye beneath the lower eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden, and I only managed to reach a small part of it, where it was fixed in its wooden shaft, and it was a large arrow from the Turkish bows, with a smooth iron head, without barbs.
Sanatus est ergo Iudaeus, et non accidit in oculo eius accidens malum.	Thus the Jew was healed, and no bad incident occurred to his eye.
Et extraxi sagittam aliam ex gutture Christiani.	And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
Et erat sagitta arabica, et est illa cui sunt aures.	And it was an Arabic arrow, one that has barbs.
Secui ergo super ipsam inter duas guidegi: et iam submersa erat in gutture eius.	I therefore made an incision over it between the two guidegi, for it was already embedded in his throat.
Subtiliatus sum ergo cum ea, donec extraxi eam: et sanatus est Christianus.	So I proceeded delicately with it until I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
Et extraxi sagittam aliam uiro quae ceciderat in uentre eius: et cogitauimus, quia moreretur ex ea.	And I extracted another arrow from a man, which had fallen into his abdomen, and we thought he would die from it.
Postquam ergo praeterierunt ei triginta dies, et non mutatum est super eum aliquid ex dispositionibus eius, secui super sagittam: et ingeniaui super eam, et extraxi eam: et sanatus est, et non accidit ei accidens malum.	But after thirty days had passed, and nothing in his condition had changed, I made an incision over the arrow, maneuvered skillfully, extracted it, and he was healed, with no bad incident occurring.
Et uidi uirum cui cecidit sagitta in dorso eius, et consolidatum est uulnus super eam.	And I saw a man into whose back an arrow had fallen, and the wound had healed over it.
Postquam ergo fuit post septem annos, exiuit sagitta in radice coxae eius.	Then, seven years later, the arrow emerged at the base of his hip.
Et uidi mulierem cui cecidit in uentre suo sagitta, et consolidatum est uulnus super eam, et remansit sagitta intra ipsam: et non mutata est ex dispositionibus eius aliqua, et mulier non inuenit ei nocumentum in aliqua ex operationibus suis naturalibus.	And I saw a woman into whose abdomen an arrow had fallen, and the wound healed over it, and the arrow remained inside her, yet none of her bodily functions were affected, and she experienced no harm in any of her natural operations.
Et uidi uirum alium, cui cecidit sagitta in facie sua: et consolidatum est uulnus, et remansit, non inueniens ei multum dolorem: et similia istis multa.	And I saw another man into whose face an arrow had fallen; the wound healed, and it remained without causing him much pain, and many other similar cases.
Et extraxi sagittam uiro ex principibus Regis, quae ceciderat ei in medio nasi sui, et iam declinauerat ad partem dextram parumper: et occultata erat sagitta tota.	And I extracted an arrow from a man among the King’s nobles, which had fallen into the middle of his nose and had shifted slightly to the right; the entire arrow was concealed.
Vocatus sum ergo ad curationem eius post casum sagittae usque ad tres dies.	Thus I was called to treat him three days after the arrow had fallen.
Inueni ergo uulnus sagittae strictum ualde.	I found the arrow wound very tight.
Inquisiui ergo eam cum tenta subtili, et non sensi eam: et ipse quidem inueniebat punctionem et dolorem sub aure sua ex parte dextra.	I therefore probed it with a thin probe, and did not feel it, but he did feel a prick and pain under his right ear.
Speraui ergo, quod erat illa punctio ex extremitate sagittae.	I then suspected that this prick was from the arrow’s tip.
Emplastraui ergo locum cum emplastro in quo erat uirtus attractiua, et digestiua, confidens apud me, quod apostemaretur locus, et appareret mihi signum sagittae, ut secarem super eam: et non accidit in loco accidens significans quod sagitta consecuta sit locum.	So I applied a plaster to the area with attracting and digesting properties, confident that the area would become inflamed, and the sign of the arrow would appear for me to cut over it; yet no indication showed that the arrow had reached that spot.
Assiduaui ergo cum emplastro super eum diebus multis: et non accidit accidens, et consolidatum est uulnus interim: et remansit infirmus de extractione eius spacio dierum, donec sensit sagittam die quodam intra nasum suum.	So I continued applying the plaster over him for many days; no incident occurred, and the wound healed in the meantime, and he remained untroubled by the extraction for a period of days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose.
Et narrauit mihi illud.	And he told me about it.
Posui ergo super illud uulnus medicinam acutam, corrosiuam diebus multis, donec apertum est: et cum tenta tentaui ipsum, et sensi extremitatem sagittae subtilem quae adhaeret in ligno.	I then applied a sharp, corrosive medicine over the wound for many days until it opened, and I probed it and felt the thin tip of the arrow where it adhered to the wood.
Deinde addidi in apertione uulneris per illam medicinam acutam, donec apparuit nobis uisibiliter extremitas sagittae: et praeteriit mihi cum eo spacium temporis circiter quatuor menses.	Then I continued to open the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow became visible to us, and approximately four months passed with him.
Deinde postquam amplificatum est uulnus, et possible fuit mihi intromittere forcipes in ipsam, et mouere ipsam, et non obediuit ad exitum: tunc non cessaui subtiliare, et ingeniare super eam cum modis ingeniorum, donec coepi super eam die quodam cum forcipibus decenter factis, secundum quod ueniet forma eorum in fine capituli, donec extraxi eam.	Then, after the wound had widened enough, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and move it, though it did not yield to extraction, I did not stop refining and devising methods over it until one day I started on it with forceps properly made, as their form will appear at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it.
Deinde restauraui uulnus.	Then I restored the wound.
Et medici quidem iudicant, quod cartilago nasi non restauratur.	And indeed, doctors judge that the cartilage of the nose cannot be restored.
Restauraui ergo ipsam, et consolidatum est uulnus, et sanatus est infirmas sanitate integra: nec nocuit ei aliquid omnino.	Nevertheless, I restored it, and the wound healed, and the patient recovered fully, with no harm done to him at all.
Et ego narro tibi qualitatem extractionis quarundam sagittarum: ut ponas illud regulam et significationem super illud quod non dixi.	And I tell you the manner of extracting certain arrows so that you may use it as a rule and guide over what I have not described.
Quoniam partes huius artis et distinctiones earum non comprehenduntur cum narratione: neque comprehendit illud liber, et artifex prudens comparat cum pauco super multum: et cum eo quod est paesens, super illud quod est absens: et inuenit operationem nouam, et instrumentum nouum apud descensus rerum extranearum huius artis quando accidunt.	For the parts of this art and their distinctions are not fully conveyed in narration, nor does a book cover it all, and a skilled craftsman compares the small over the large, and what is present over what is absent, and finds a new procedure and new instrument in the context of extraordinary occurrences in this art when they happen.
Dico ergo, quod sagittae non egrediuntur ex membris quibus sunt fixae, nisi secundum duos modos.	Therefore, I say that arrows do not exit from the parts in which they are fixed except in two ways.
Aut per attractionem ex loco ex quo intrauit.	Either by drawing it out from the place where it entered.
Aut contrario partis alterius.	Or through the opposite side.
Et illa quae egreditur unde intrauit, aut est sagitta procedens in loco carnoso: et attrahitur, et egreditur.	And the one that exits from where it entered, or is an arrow lodged in a fleshy area, can be drawn out and extracted.
Quod si non obedit ad exitum ex hora in qua cadit: tunc oportet ut dimittas ipsam aliquot diebus, donec putrefiat caro quae est in circumitu eius: et alleuietur attractio eius, et eius egressio.	But if it does not yield to exit at the time it falls, then you should leave it for a few days, until the flesh around it begins to decompose, and the extraction and removal become easier.
Et similiter si figitur in osse, et non obedit tibi ad exitum, tunc dimitte ipsam etiam aliquot diebus: et itera eam cum attractione et motione omni die, donec egreditur.	Similarly, if it is lodged in the bone and does not yield to you for removal, then leave it also for a few days, and try again each day with traction and movement until it exits.
Quod si non obedit tibi ad exitum post dies, tunc oportet ut perfores in circumitu sagittae in ipso osse ab omni parte cum terebro subtili, donec amplificentur sagittae.	But if it still does not yield to exit after days, then you must drill around the arrow in the bone from all sides with a fine drill, until the area for the arrow is widened.
Deinde attrahe eam, et extrahe ipsam.	Then pull it and extract it.
Si autem sagitta est fixa in capitis osse, et iam processit in uno uentrium cerebri, et apparent ex infirmo quaedam illorum accidentium quae dixi: tunc abstine ab attractione sagittae, et dimitte eam, donec aequetur esse eius post dies.	If, however, the arrow is fixed in the bone of the head and has already entered one of the brain’s ventricles, and some of the symptoms I mentioned appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it, until the condition stabilizes after some days.
Nam si sagitta peruenit ad sifach, tunc mors non prolongatur ei.	For if the arrow reaches the membrane (sifach), then death is not delayed for him.
Et si sagitta non est fixa, nisi in corpore ossis tantum, et non penetrat usque ad sifach: et remanet infirmus aliquot diebus, et non accidit ei ex illis accidentibus aliquid: tunc ingenia in attractione sagittae et extractione eius.	But if the arrow is only fixed in the bone itself, and does not penetrate to the membrane, and the patient remains stable for some days without any of those symptoms occurring, then proceed with ingenuity in pulling and extracting the arrow.
Si autem est fixa ualde, et non obedit tibi ad attractionem: tunc administra terebella in circumitu sagittae, sicut narraui tibl: deinde cura locum, donec sanetur.	If it is firmly fixed and does not yield to traction, then use drills around the arrow as I told you, and then treat the area until it heals.
Si autem Sagitta iam absconsa est in loco corporis, et occultatur et absconditur a sensu: tunc inquire eam cum tenta.	If the arrow is already hidden in a part of the body and is concealed and out of reach, then locate it with a probe.
Si autem sentis eam, tunc attrahe eam cum quibusdam instrumentis quae conueniunt attractioni eius.	If you feel it, then pull it with certain instruments suitable for its extraction.
Si autem non potes super eam, propter constrictionem uulneris, et propter longitudinem sagittae in profundo: et non est illic os, neque neruus, neque uena: tunc finde super eam, donec amplificetur uulnus, et sit possibile extrahere sagittam.	If you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and if there is no bone, nerve, or vein there, then make an incision over it, widening the wound until it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
Quod si sunt ei aures quibus teneatur, tunc libera carnem fixam in eis ab omni parte, omni ingenio quo potes illud, et ingenia, si non potest super euasionem carnis in fractura aurium, et contorsione earum, donec euadat.	If it has barbs to hold it, then free the flesh lodged in them on all sides with every skill you can manage, and proceed carefully if the flesh cannot yield to the breaking of the barbs or their twisting, until it is freed.
Et tu quando administras extractionem Sagittae, in quocumque loco est, tunc utere contorsione manus tuae, cum forficibus ad partes omnes, donec facias eam euadere: et subtilia ultima subtilitate, ut non frangatur Sagita, et fiat difficilis tibi attractio eius, et ipsius extractio.	And when you are performing the extraction of an arrow, wherever it is, use the twisting of your hand with forceps in all directions until you make it emerge, handling it with the utmost delicacy so that the arrow does not break, making its extraction more difficult for you.
Quod si non potes super eam ex hora una, tunc dimitte eam diebus aliquot, donec putrefiant quae sunt in circumitu eius.	If you cannot manage it in one attempt, then leave it for several days, until the surrounding tissues begin to decay.
Deinde itera eam: alleuietur enim tuncia Si uero contrarietatem tibi facit fluxus sanguinis, tunc administra quod diximus ex curatione in capitulo suo: et custodi uirtutem tuam ab incisione uenae, aut nerui, aut chordae.	Then try again, as it will be easier. If, however, bleeding hinders you, then administer the treatment I mentioned in its chapter, and take care to avoid cutting any vein, nerve, or tendon.
Et administra ingenium omni modo quo tibi possibile est euasionis Sagittae: et sit illud cum facilitate, et tranquillitate, uel tarditate, et firmitate, sicut narraui tibi.	And use every skill you can in freeing the arrow, with ease, calm, or slowness, and firmness, as I have told you.
Et oportet, ut facias cum trahis Sagittam, ut infirmus sit secundum figuram, secundum quam fuit apud casum Sagittae in ipso.	And it is necessary that when you pull the arrow, the patient is positioned as he was at the moment the arrow struck him.
Quoniam est magis iuuatiuum.	For it is more beneficial.
Si autem non est tibi possibile illud, tunc administra quod tibi est possibile ex figuris.	But if that is not possible, then use whatever positions are available to you.
Sagitta autem quae egreditur ex contrario partis alterius, aut iam procedit ex ea aliquid ad exteriora: aut facit gibbositatem extremitas Sagittae per sensum ex superiore parte cutis propinquae, et uides eam eminentem: seca ergo super eam, et sit sectio secundum quantitatem quod capiant in ea forcipes.	Now, for an arrow that exits through the opposite side, either part of it is already protruding externally, or its tip creates a bulge felt under the nearby surface of the skin, and you see it protruding; make an incision over it large enough to accommodate the forceps.
Deinde attrahe eam: ipsa namque fit facilis ad exitum.	Then pull it, for it will be easy to remove.
Si uero retinetur in osse, tunc torque manum tuam secundum rotunditatem, donec imprimat Sagitta in osse, et amplificet sibiipsi: deinde trahe ipsam.	But if it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand in a circular motion until the arrow is set in the bone and enlarges itself: then pull it out.
Et si non: dimitte eam diebus aliquot: deinde itera eam, donec egrediatur.	And if not: leave it for a few days, then try again until it comes out.
Quod si lignum Sagittae est in ea, tunc impelle ipsam cum eo.	But if the shaft of the arrow is within it, then push it with it.
Si autem cecidit lignum, et uis administrare impulsionem: tunc intromitte ad eam instrumentum concauum, ut ingrediatur concauitas eius in caudam Sagittae: deinde impelle eam cum ipso.	But if the shaft has fallen, and you wish to apply pressure, then insert into it a hollow instrument so that its concavity enters the tail of the arrow: then push it with it.
Si autem Sagita est concaua, tunc impelle eam cum instrumento intrante in ipsam.	But if the arrow is hollow, then push it with an instrument that enters it.
Sagitta enim alleuiatur per illud.	For the arrow is eased by that.
Quod si Sagitta est toxicata, tunc oportet, ut concaues carnem totam, in qua est uenenum: si est tibi possibile illud.	But if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to hollow out all the flesh in which the poison is, if that is possible for you.
Deinde cura eam cum eo quod conuenit ad illud.	Then treat it with what is suitable for it.
Quod si Sagitta est cadens in pectore, aut in uentre, aut in uesica, aut in latere: et est propinqua ex eo quod tangis cum tenta, et possibile est tibi secare super eam: tunc seca, et caue ab incisione uenae, aut nerui, et extrahe eam.	But if the arrow has fallen into the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side, and it is near enough that you can touch it with the probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and beware of cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it.
Deinde sue uulnus, si uides, et cura illud, donec sanetur.	Then suture the wound if you see fit, and treat it until it heals.
Et haec est forma Forcipum, quibus extrahuntur Sagittae.	And this is the form of the forceps by which arrows are extracted.
Sint extremitates similes rostro auis, quae factae sunt sicut ferra: quando capiunt super Sagittam, non dimittunt eam.	Let the tips be like a bird’s beak, which are made like iron: when they grip the arrow, they do not let go.
Et fiunt ex eis species multae, magnae, et paruae, et mediae, secundum quantitatem magnitudinis Sagittae, et paruitatis eius, et amplitudinis uulneris, et constrictionis eius.	And many types are made from them, large, small, and medium, according to the size of the arrow and its slenderness, the breadth of the wound, and its narrowness.
Forma Impellentis concaui.	The shape of the hollow pusher.
Et forma Impellentis surdi.	And the shape of the blunt pusher.
Hoc est surdae extremitatis sicut Radius, ut alleuietur introitus eius in sagittam concauam, et impellatur cum eo.	This is of a blunt end like a spoke, so that its entry into the hollow arrow is eased and it is pushed with it.
Huius extremitas impellentis concauae est concaua sicut penna auis: ut alleuietur introitus sagittae in ipsam et impulsio eius.	The tip of this hollow pusher is hollow like a bird's feather, so that the entry of the arrow into it is eased, as is its pushing.
¶ De phlebotomia Venarum.	On the bloodletting of veins.
Caput XCVII.	Chapter 97.
Uenae phlebotomandae, quarum cucurrit consuetudo in corpore, sunt triginta.	The veins to be bloodlet, which are customary in the body, are thirty.
Ex quibus in capite sunt sexdecim: duae Venae pulsatiles, quae sunt post aures, dictae duae Herbae: et duae Arteriae, quae sunt in duobus timporibus apparentes: et duae Venae quae sunt in duobus lachrymalibus duorum oculorum, quae dicuntur Aspicientes: et Vena praeparata in medio frontis: et uena quae est in extremitate nasi.	Of these, sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins behind the ears, called the two "Herbs"; two arteries visible in the temples; two veins in the tear ducts of the two eyes, called "Lookers"; a vein situated in the middle of the forehead; and a vein at the tip of the nose.
Et duae Guidegi, quae sunt in collo.	And two "Guidegi" veins in the neck.
Et duae uenae quae sunt in labio superiore oris.	And two veins on the upper lip of the mouth.
Et duae uenae quae sunt in labio inferiore.	And two veins on the lower lip.
Et istae sunt uenae quae dicuntur Algecherich.	And these are the veins called "Algecherich."
Et duae uenae quae sunt sub lingua.	And two veins under the tongue.
Venae autem quae phlebotomantur in brachiis, sunt quinque uenae in omni brachio.	However, in the arms, there are five veins in each arm to be bloodlet.
Vna earum est Cephalica, et est ex latere syluestri: et nominat eam uulgus uenam capitis.	One of them is the Cephalic, located on the outer side; the common people call it the "head vein."
Et Fusca.	And the Dark vein.
Et est Media uena, et est composita ex ramo Basilicae, et ramo Cephalicae: et nominat eam uulgus uenam corporis.	And there is the Middle vein, formed from a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic; the common people call it the "body vein."
Et Basilica, et est posita in latere domestico: et nominatur etiam Titillicaris.	And the Basilic, positioned on the inner side, also called "Titillicaris."
Et nominat eam uulgus uenam uentris.	And the common people call it the "belly vein."
Et Funis brachii, et est posita super focile: et est illa in qua phlebotomatur, et est illa quae apparet supra pollicem appatitione manifesta.	And the Cord of the arm, positioned above the elbow joint; this is the one bloodlet, visible above the thumb with clear appearance.
Et Saluatella: et est uena quae est inter annularem et minimum: et sunt ei duo rami.	And the Salvatic vein, located between the ring finger and the little finger, with two branches.
Et in crure et in pede sunt tres uenae.	In the leg and foot, there are three veins.
Vna earum est quae est sub pollicis curuatura ex latere syluestri.	One of these is located under the arch of the big toe on the outer side.
Et secunda est Saphena: et locus eius est apud calcaneum ex latere domestico.	The second is the Saphenous, located near the heel on the inner side.
Et uena Nesse: et locus eius est apud calcaneum ex latere syluestri.	And the Nesse vein, located near the heel on the outer side.
Et in crure alio sunt tres uenae similes illis.	And in the other leg, there are three veins similar to these.
¶ De phlebotomia duarum uenarum post Aures.	On the bloodletting of the two veins behind the ears.
Phlebotomiae uero duarum uenarum quae sunt post Aures, iuuamentum est ad catarros antiquos, et hemigraneam, et sahafati, et ulcera capitis mala antiqua.	Bloodletting of the two veins behind the ears is beneficial for chronic catarrh, migraines, "sahafati," and old sores of the head.
Et qualitas phlebotomandi eam est secundum quod narro.	And the method of bloodletting it is as I will describe.
Et est, ut radatur caput infirmi: et fricetur occipitium eius in loco duarum uenarum cum panno aspero fricatione bona.	The patient’s head is to be shaved, and the occiput is rubbed at the location of the two veins with a rough cloth, rubbing well.
Deinde stringat infirmus collum suum cum uitta, donec appareant duae uenae.	Then the patient should tightly bind his neck with a band until the two veins appear.
Et loca earum sunt post Aures in duobus locis depressis capitis.	Their locations are behind the ears in two sunken spots of the head.
Quaere ergo eas digito tuo, et ubi sentis pulsum earum sub digito tuo, illic signa cum encaustro.	Therefore, locate them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark the spot with ink.
Deinde accipe phlebotomum cultellarem, et est ille qui dicitur Incidens.	Then take the lancet knife, called the "Incisor."
Deinde intromitte ipsum sub uena in cute, donec perueniat phlebotomus ad os.	Then insert it under the vein in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone.
Deinde eleua manum tuam cum uena et cute ad superiora, et incide uenam cum cute incisione incidente.	Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein along with the skin with a cutting incision.
Et sit longitudo incisionis quantitas duorum digitorum iunctorum, aut circiter illud: et fac exire ex sanguine quantum uis.	The length of the incision should be about the width of two joined fingers, or around that; let as much blood flow as you desire.
Deinde stringe eam cum puluillis, et dimitte eam, donec sanetur.	Then compress it with pads, and leave it until it heals.
Et iam praecessit in principio libri incisio earum, et ipsarum cauterizatio.	And their incision and cauterization were already covered at the beginning of the book.
¶ De phlebotomia duarum Arteriarum in timporibus.	On the bloodletting of the two arteries in the temples.
Duarum uero Arteriarum quae sunt in duobus timporibus phlebotomiae iuuamentum, est ad hemigraneam antiquam: et sodam prauam, et obtalmiam assiduam: et cursum superfluitatum effusarum acutarum ad oculos.	Bloodletting of the two arteries in the temples is beneficial for chronic migraines, a persistent throbbing, continuous eye pain, and the flow of excessive discharges to the eyes.
Et qualitas phlebotomandi eas est secundum quod narro tibi.	And the method of bloodletting them is as I will describe to you.
Stringat infirmus collum suum multum, donec appareant duae uenae sensui apparitione manifesta: et demonstretur pulsus earum sub digito tuo.	The patient should tightly bind his neck until the two veins become clearly visible to the touch, and their pulse is felt under your finger.
Tunc enim signa cum encaustro.	Then mark them with ink.
Deinde eleua cutem desuper uenam ad superiora cum digito tuo indice, et intromitte phlebotomum incidentem desubtus, et eleua uenam ad superiora.	Then lift the skin above the vein upward with your index finger, insert the cutting lancet underneath, and raise the vein upwards.
Et incide eam, sicut fecisti in duabus uenis aliis: et fac currere ex sanguine secundum quantitatem necessitatis tuae.	And cut it as you did with the other two veins, allowing the blood to flow according to your need.
Deinde solue coartationem infirmi, et pone digitum tuum super uenam hora una.	Then release the patient’s binding and press your finger over the vein for an hour.
Postea pone super ipsam cotum et puluillos, et stringe ipsum desuper constrictione forti: et dimitte ipsam, donec sanetur.	Afterward, place a dressing and pads on it, and bind it tightly from above; leave it until it heals.
Et iam praecessit rememoratio earum, et ipsarum incisio, et earum extractio in principio libri.	And their mention, incision, and extraction were already covered at the beginning of the book.
¶ De phlebotomia uenae frontis post phlebotomiam Cephalicae.	On the bloodletting of the frontal vein after the bloodletting of the Cephalic.
Iuuamentum uero phlebotomiae uenae frontis est post phlebotomiam Cephalicae, ad aegritudines faciei antiquas: et ad hemigraneam, et rubedinem: et ulcera foeda.	The benefit of bloodletting the frontal vein after the bloodletting of the Cephalic is for old ailments of the face, for migraines, redness, and foul ulcers.
Et qualitas phlebotomiae eius est, secundum quod narro tibi.	And the method of its bloodletting is as I will tell you.
Coartet infirmus collum suum cum uitta, donec appareat uena.	The patient should tightly bind his neck with a band until the vein appears.
Deinde accipe instrumentum quod nominatur Fossorium.	Then take the instrument called the "Digger."
Cuius haec est forma.	Whose shape is as follows.
Pone acumen eminens quod est in capite Fossorii super ipsam uenam, et percute super ipsum cum pectine, aut cum re alia in modo eius: et dimitte sanguinem currere secundum quantitatem quam uis.	Place the pointed tip that is on the head of the Digger over the vein, and strike it with a comb or something similar, and let the blood flow according to the quantity you desire.
Deinde solue perforationem infirmi, et stringe eam, donec sanetur.	Then release the perforation of the patient, and press it until it heals.
Et quandoque aperitur cum phlebotomo lato.	Sometimes it is opened with a broad lancet.
Verumtamen non oportet, ut sit phlebotomus acutae extremitatis, sicut reliqui phlebotomi, imo sit latae extremitatis.	However, it is not necessary for the lancet to have a sharp end like other lancets; rather, it should have a broad end.
Et phlebotoma eam secundum transuersionem: quoniam os est propinquum: et fortasse in eo frangetur phlebotomus quando est subtilis.	And bloodlet it across, since the bone is close, and perhaps the lancet might break in it if it is fine.
¶ De phlebotomia Venarum in duobus Lachrymalibus.	On the bloodletting of the veins in the two tear ducts.
Iuuamentum autem duarum Venarum quae sunt in duobus Lachrymalibus duorum oculorum, est ad oculum ex scabie et rubedine: et sebel in oculo, et aegritudinibus faciei.	The benefit of the two veins in the two tear ducts of the eyes is for the eye suffering from scabies and redness, and "sebel" in the eye, and ailments of the face.
Et qualitas phlebotomiae earum est, ut stringat infirmus collum suum cum uitta.	The method of their bloodletting is for the patient to tightly bind his neck with a band.
Deinde phlebotoma eas: et tu sis stans super caput eius.	Then bloodlet them while you stand above his head.
Et fiat phlebotomia secundum transuersum ad longitudinem parum, cum phlebotomo paruo, lato parumper, nam in loco non est caro.	And the bloodletting should be done horizontally in a short length, with a small lancet, somewhat broad, as there is no flesh in that place.
Si enim phlebotomus est subtilis extremitatis, fortasse frangitur.	If the lancet has a fine end, it may break.
Deinde fac currere ex sanguine quantum est tibi necesse.	Then let as much blood flow as you need.
Et pone super eas cotum, et stringe eas nocte una, et solue eas.	And place a dressing on them, bind them for one night, then release them.
¶ De phlebotomia uenarum Nasi.	On the bloodletting of the nasal veins.
Venae autem Nasi phlebotomia, est conferens ad febrem acutam, et sodam uehementem, et aegritudines faciei, sicut hemigraneam, et rubedinem quae accidit in facie: praecipue quando est antiqua.	The bloodletting of the nasal veins is helpful for acute fever, severe throbbing, and ailments of the face, like migraines and redness that appears in the face, especially when it is old.
Et qualitas phlebotomiae eius est, ut stringat infirmus collum suum.	The method of its bloodletting is for the patient to tightly bind his neck.
Deinde tene nasum eius manu tua sinistra, et accipe phlebotomum subtilem, longum: et fige eum in medio leporis ipsius, inter illud quod distinguit nasum secundum rectitudinem.	Then hold his nose with your left hand, take a fine, long lancet, and fix it in the middle of the cartilage, between what divides the nose straight down.
Quoniam uena non apparet sensui illic, et quia sanguis incipit fluere statim.	For the vein is not felt there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
Et oportet, ut ultimes manum tuam cum phlebotomo parumper: et facias currere ex sanguine secundum necessitatem tuam.	And it is necessary to hold your hand with the lancet for a short time, and let the blood flow according to your need.
Deinde liga ipsam nocte una.	Then bind it for one night.
Ipsa enim restauratur uelociter.	For it heals quickly.
¶ De phlebotomia uenarum Guidegi.	On the bloodletting of the Guidegi veins.
Duarum uero Guidegi phlebotomiae iuuamentum, est ad constrictionem anhelitus, et incaeptionem leprae, et aegritudines melancholicas, et impetiginem et ulcera mala, et corrodentibus, id est, herpetibus estiomenis.	The benefit of bloodletting the two Guidegi veins is for constriction of breath, the onset of leprosy, melancholic ailments, impetigo, foul ulcers, and corrosive conditions, that is, scaly herpes.
Et qualitas phlebotomiae earum est ut stringat infirmus sub utrisque in collo suo cum ligamento: et stet artifex super caput infirmi, et infirmus sit sedens super sedem.	The method of their bloodletting is for the patient to bind under both in his neck with a strap, and for the practitioner to stand above the patient’s head while the patient sits on a seat.
Deinde phlebotoma uenam ad longitudinem phlebotomia ampla parumper.	Then bloodlet the vein in a somewhat large length.
Deinde extrahe ex sanguine quantitatem temperatam, aut secundum illud quod uides ex necessitate quae est ad illud.	Then extract a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see is needed.
Deinde fac similiter de uena alia.	Then do likewise with the other vein.
Postea solue ligamentum, et stringe duas uenas strictura media: ut non praefocetur infirmus.	Afterward, release the binding and press the two veins with moderate pressure so that the patient is not suffocated.
Et dimitte eam usque mane.	And leave it until morning.
Sanatur enim uulnus.	For the wound heals.
¶ De phlebotomia uenarum Algeberich.	On the bloodletting of the Algeberich veins.
Venae autem Algeberich phlebotomiae iuuamentum est: quia confert ad alcolam in ore, et corruptionem gingiuae: et ulcera mala: et fissuras labiorum, et ulcera mala, etiam quae sunt in naso, et in circumitu eius.	The benefit of bloodletting the Algeberich veins is that it helps with the ulcerations in the mouth, the corruption of the gums, foul ulcers, cracks in the lips, and even foul ulcers in and around the nose.
Et qualitas phlebotomiae earum est, ut facias sedere infirmum coram te: et stringe collum eius cum uitta.	The method of bloodletting them is to have the patient sit before you and to bind his neck with a band.
Deinde reuolue labium eius, et aspice ad duas uenas: quarum una est a dextera labii, et secunda a sinistra eius: et demonstratio utriusque est per nigredinem ambarum.	Then turn his lip, and look at the two veins, one on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, both identified by their dark color.
Et illud uerum est: quia in circumitu earum sunt uenae minutae, nigrae.	And that is true, as around them are tiny, dark veins.
Incide ergo utramque incisione incidente.	Then make an incision in each one.
Si autem dubitatio est super te, et non scis quae earum est: tunc intende ad incisionem maioris earum, et ipsarum manifestiorem.	But if you are in doubt and do not know which one, then focus on the incision of the larger and more visible one.
Et similiter fac de duabus uenis quae sunt in labio superiore: et plurimum quidem currit consuetudo cum eo: et incide duas uenas quae sunt in labio inferiori.	Similarly, do the same with the two veins in the upper lip; this is often customary, and incise the two veins in the lower lip.
¶ De phlebotomia Venae sub lingua post Cephalicae phlebotomiam.	On the bloodletting of the vein under the tongue after the Cephalic bloodletting.
Duarum uero Venarum quae sunt sub lingua phlebotomiae iuuamentum: est, quod phlebotomia Cephalicae est ad praefocationes quae sunt in gutture, et aegritudinem uuulae, et aegritudines oris.	The benefit of bloodletting the two veins under the tongue is that the Cephalic bloodletting helps with obstructions in the throat, ailments of the uvula, and diseases of the mouth.
Et qualitas phlebotomandi eas est, ut sedeat infirmus inter manus tuas coram sole: et eleualinguam eius, et aspice sub lingua a latere eius uno uenam, et a latere eius alio uenam: et color utriusque decliuis ad nigredinem.	The method of bloodletting them is to have the patient sit between your hands facing the sun, lift his tongue, and look under the tongue for a vein on each side, both with a darkish color.
Phlebotoma ergo utramque: et custodi, ne ultimes manum tuam in phlebotomando eas.	Therefore, bloodlet each one, and take care not to press your hand too much in bloodletting them.
Nam sub eius sunt arteriae, et fortasse accidet fluxus sanguinis ex illis arteriis.	For beneath them are arteries, and there may be a blood flow from those arteries.
¶ De tribus Venis in cubito.	On the three veins in the elbow.
Venae autem tres quae phlebotomantur in cubito, sunt in quarum phlebotomia cucurrit consuetudo in omnibus hominibus.	The three veins in the elbow that are bloodlet are commonly treated in all people.
Et phlebotomia earum fit secundum duos modos.	Their bloodletting is done in two ways.
Aut pungendo cum phlebotomo myrtino lato: aut oliuari ad subtilitatem decliui: aut secando cum phlebotomo cultellari, et est Alnesil.	Either by pricking with a broad myrtle-shaped lancet, or an olive-shaped one with a fine edge, or by cutting with a knife-shaped lancet, called "Alnesil."
Et haec est forma eius.	And this is its shape.
Sic Alnesil est, quod conuenit ad sectionem.	Thus, the Alnesil is suitable for incisions.
Et ex eis sunt species latae, et subtiles secundum amplitudinem uenarum et stricturam earum Forma phlebotomi myrtini sit lata, sicut uides.	And among them are types that are broad and fine, according to the width of the veins and their narrowness. The shape of the myrtle-shaped lancet should be broad, as you see.
Et conuenit ad aperiendas uenas concauas, plenas, procedentes, apparentes, grossas, et illas quae continent sanguinem grossum, turbidum.	And it is suitable for opening hollow, full, projecting, visible, thick veins that contain thick, turbid blood.
Et iste phlebotomus est minoris latitudinis, et subtilioris extreminatis.	And this lancet is of lesser width and a finer tip.
Et conuenit ad phlebotomiam uenarum subtilium, quae continent sanguinem subtilem citrinum.	And it is suitable for the bloodletting of fine veins that contain thin, yellowish blood.
¶ De phlebotomia Basilicae.	On the bloodletting of the Basilica vein.
Basilicae autem quae est una harum trium uenarum phlebotomiae iuuamentum est.	The bloodletting of the Basilica vein, which is one of these three veins, is beneficial.
Quia extrahit sanguinem ex aegritudinibus, quae sunt sub gutture et collo, ex eo quod sequitur pectus, et uentrem: et conuenit phlebotomatori cum phlebotomat eam, ut caueat, et sit super timorem ex ea nam sub ea est arteria.	It draws blood from ailments that are below the throat and neck, following down to the chest and abdomen. The bloodletter must be cautious when bloodletting it, as there is an artery beneath it.
Si ergo errat, et addit in punctura phlebotomi, incidet illam arteriam, et accidet fluxus sanguinis.	If an error is made, and too much force is applied in the lancet puncture, it will cut that artery, resulting in a blood flow.
Quapropter oportet, ne phlebotomet eam cum phlebotomo pungente: imo sit secans cum Alnesil.	Therefore, it is necessary not to use a puncturing lancet but rather to cut with the Alnesil.
Quod si non apparet Basilica apparitione manifesta, tunc oportet, ut alienes ipsam, et permuteris ad aliam, aut quaeras aliquem ramum eius: aut phlebotomes loco eius Funem brachii.	If the Basilica is not clearly visible, then it is necessary to change to another vein or seek one of its branches, or instead bloodlet the Cord of the arm.
Ipsa enim est manifesta, et seca eam cum phlebotomo Anesil, sicut diximus.	The Cord of the arm is clearly visible, so cut it with the Alnesil, as we mentioned.
Si autem uis phlebotomiam ipsius eiusdem, tunc oportet ante stricturam brachii, ut tangas locum, donec cognoscas locum pulsus.	If you wish to bloodlet the Basilica itself, you should press the arm beforehand to feel for the pulse location.
Deinde signa super locum cum encaustro, postea brachium, et seca uenam sectione concaua cum phlebotomo Alnesil, sicut diximus.	Then mark the spot with ink, bind the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using the Alnesil, as we described.
Et caue ne cadat percussio per spacium a loco arteriae.	Be cautious not to let the cut fall near the artery.
Et quando uides, cum tu stringis ligamen, inflationem in loco quem signasti cum encaustro, tunc illa inflatio est inflatio arteriae: deuita ergo eam.	And if you see an inflation at the marked spot when you bind it, that inflation is from the artery; therefore, avoid it.
Quod si uides sanguinem apud phlebotomiam salire, sicut urina pueri, et est sanguis subtilis, rubeus: tunc scias, quia est ex sanguine arteriae.	If you see the blood spurting at the bloodletting site like a child’s urine, and it is thin, red blood, then know that it is from the artery.
Tunc ergo propera, et pone digitum tuum super ipsum spacio longo, deinde aufer digitum tuum.	Then act quickly and place your finger on it for a long moment, then remove your finger.
Si ergo abscinditur sanguis (multotiens namque abscinditur) tunc stringe brachium, et dimitte ipsum: et caueat infirmus a motione eius: et sit super timorem eius.	If the blood flow stops (for it often stops), then bind the arm, and let it be, advising the patient to refrain from moving it and to be cautious.
Et non moueat ipsum diebus aliquot, donec sanetur.	And he should not move it for a few days until it heals.
Si autem non abscinditur sanguis, et uincit te: et non est tibi praesens in momento tuo medicina, tunc incide arteriam, si apparet tibi.	But if the blood does not stop and overwhelms you, and no remedy is at hand, then cut the artery if you can see it.
Nam extremitates eius contrahuntur, et abscinditur sanguis.	For its ends will contract, stopping the blood.
Aut accipe corticem fistici, et finde eum: accipe medietatem unam, et stringe eam super locum uenae strictura decenti, cum ligamento et puluillis usque ad diem alium.	Or take the bark of a pistachio tree, split it, take one half, and bind it over the vein with appropriate pressure using a band and pads until the next day.
Tunc si abscinditur sanguis, est bonum.	Then if the blood stops, it is good.
Et si non: tunc cura ipsum cum eo cuius praecessit rememoratio ex loco puluerum abscindentium fluxum sanguinis: et abscisio sanguinis eius non est difficilis in pluribus dispositionibus, propter locum paruum uulneris, et possibilitatem ligamenti ex brachio.	And if not, then treat it with what has previously been mentioned from the location of the powders that stop blood flow; stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases due to the small wound and the possibility of binding from the arm.
¶ De phlebotomia uenae Fuscae.	On the bloodletting of the Fusca vein.
Venae autem Fuscae phlebotomiae iuuamentum est, quod trahit sanguinem ex superiori parte capitis, et inferiore corporis: propterea, quia est composita ex ramo Basilicae, et ramo Cephalicae, sicut diximus.	The bloodletting of the Fusca vein is beneficial as it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body, due to its composition from a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalic, as we mentioned.
Et conuenit phlebotomanti eam, ut sit super timorem ex phlebotomia eius, nam sub ea est neruus.	The bloodletter should exercise caution in bloodletting it, as there is a nerve beneath it.
Si ergo addit in punctura phlebotomi, et inuenit neruum, accidit in ea stupor, et difficilis fit sanatio eius, et fortasse non sanatur penitus.	If too much force is applied in the lancet puncture and the nerve is struck, numbness will occur, and healing will be difficult, perhaps even incomplete.
Et iste neruus multotiens apparet sensui.	This nerve is often felt.
Si autem occultatur in quibusdam hominibus, et est subtilis, et non ostenditur: tunc oportet ut ponas phlebotomiam tuam qua eam phlebotomas, sectionem cum Alnesil, et deuita neruum cum fortitudine tua.	If it is hidden in some people and is fine and not visible, then it is necessary to position your bloodletting with the Alnesil and avoid the nerve with caution.
Quod si uena est inter duos neruos, tunc finde uenam in longitudine.	If the vein lies between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise.
¶ De uena Cephalica.	On the Cephalic vein.
Phlebotomiae autem uenae Cephalicae iuuamentum est, quia attrahit sanguinem ex capite, et confert ad aegritudines oculorum.	The bloodletting of the Cephalic vein is beneficial as it draws blood from the head and helps with ailments of the eyes.
Et oportet in hac uena proprie, si uis, ut phlebotomes eam pungendo cum phlebotomo oliuari, aut cum phlebotomo myrtino: quoniam est saluior uenarum omnium, quoniam non est subea arteria, neque neruus.	And it is appropriate with this vein, if you wish, to bloodlet it by pricking with an olive-shaped or myrtle-shaped lancet, as it is the safest of all veins, as there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
Verumtamen conuenit tibi apud phlebotomiam, ut deuites cum phlebotomo caput lacerti tantum, et quaeras locum leniorem: et non nocet ei, si non inuenitur cum percussione prima, ut iteretur super eam cum phlebotomia aliquotiens.
